    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_910977087" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/910977087" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/910977087?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9529625-dr-jekyll-and-mr-hyde">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-q-t">read-authors-q-t</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=ghost-horror-supernatural">ghost-horror-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=19th-century-ish">19th-century-ish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer910977087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer752385113579625459">Do you know what a "Jekyll and Hyde" character is? Of course you do. It is one of the descriptions, originally in a piece of literature, which has now become accepted in our vernacular. And there are many renditions of the story, <b>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</b>, and countless references to it in all aspects of life. Quite an achievement for a slim Victorian volume written by the Scottish author Robert Louis Stevenson, and published in 1886.<br/><br/><i>"Man is not truly one, but truly two."</i><br/><br/>So ass</span>
  <span id="freeText752385113579625459" style="display:none">Do you know what a "Jekyll and Hyde" character is? Of course you do. It is one of the descriptions, originally in a piece of literature, which has now become accepted in our vernacular. And there are many renditions of the story, <b>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</b>, and countless references to it in all aspects of life. Quite an achievement for a slim Victorian volume written by the Scottish author Robert Louis Stevenson, and published in 1886.<br /><br /><i>"Man is not truly one, but truly two."</i><br /><br />So asserts Dr. Jekyll. But we are slightly handicapped nowadays by knowing the crux of the plot beforehand. Before this tale there seems to have been nothing similar, although there had been earlier tales in literature about doppelgängers. Robert Louis Stevenson had always been interested in the duality of human nature, and shown admiration for morally ambiguous heroes - or anti-heroes. But the spark which produced this novel was ignited by a dream he had had. His wife Fanny reported,<br /><br /><i>"In the small hours of one morning ... I was awakened by cries of horror from Louis. Thinking he had a nightmare, I awakened him. He said angrily, 'Why did you wake me? I was dreaming a fine bogey tale.' I had awakened him at the first transformation scene."</i><br /><br />The writing of the story itself is a gripping tale. Stevenson wrote the original draft with feverish excitement, taking less than three days. He then collapsed with a haemorrhage, and his wife edited the manuscript, as was her habit. The story is that it was she who suggested to her husband that he should have written it as an allegory, rather than a story.<br /><br />On being left alone with his manuscript, Stevenson promptly burnt it to ashes, thus forcing himself to start again from scratch, and rewrite it in the form of an allegory. It is unclear whether this is true, or myth, since there can be no evidence of a burnt manuscript. However later biographers of Robert Louis Stevenson have claimed that he was probably on drugs such as cocaine when writing it. He was certainly ill and confined to bed at the time.<br /><br /><b>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</b> was an immediate success, and remains Stevenson's most popular work. It is only recently however that his work has been thought to deserve critical attention. The author himself took his writing lightly, shrugging his popularity off with a dismissive,<br /><br /><i>"Fiction is to grown men what play is to the child,"</i><br /><br />and continuing to write his swashbuckling stories of romance and adventure; what he called <i>"historical tushery."</i><br /><br /><b>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</b> was thus an unusual tale for him to write. Perhaps its popularity at the time was partly due to its high moral tone. Not only was it adapted for the stage, but was also said to be widely quoted in religious sermons. <br /><br /><i>"With every day, and from both sides of my intelligence, the moral and the intellectual, I thus drew steadily nearer to the truth, by whose partial discovery I have been doomed to such a dreadful shipwreck: that man is not truly one, but truly two."<br /><br />"All human beings, as we meet them, are commingled out of good and evil: and Edward Hyde, alone, in the ranks of mankind, was pure evil."</i><br /><br />One can see how ministers of the church would be tempted to use the story as a convenient illustration for descriptions of temptation, sin and depravity.<br /><br />From a modern point of view the style is dated, and almost archaic. There is a lot of preamble and dissembling. Of course this must have added to the mystery. Yet since there is little mystery at all to a modern reader, it is difficult to judge. <br /><br />The novel starts with a London lawyer named Gabriel John Utterson who is intrigued to be told stories of his old friend, Dr. Henry Jekyll, and also about some evil crimes committed by a man called Edward Hyde. He himself witnesses Hyde going into Jekyll's house, describing Hyde as a <i>"troglodyte"</i>, or ugly animalistic creature. As the story moves on, we learn that not only is Hyde primitive, but also immoral, taking a delight in his crimes. He is not an animal, amoral and innocent, but a person Utterson sees as evil and depraved, full of rage and revelling in his vices. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The two violent crimes which Hyde indulges in are both directed against the most vulnerable members of society - a young child and a much-loved old man. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> The puzzle remains what could possibly be the link between the two very different men. <br /><br />Yet is the morality of civilised people merely a veneer after all? The story is set very firmly in its time, when the ideas of what was decent and upright behaviour was set, not fluid. Yet even so, appearances and facades were often just an illusory surface, hiding a more sordid truth. A respectable man would sometimes prefer to look the other way and remain ignorant, <br /><br /><i>"I feel very strongly about putting questions; it partakes too much of the style of the day of judgement. You start a question, and it's like starting a stone. You sit quietly on the top of a hill; and away the stone goes, starting others; and presently some bland old bird (the last you would have thought of) is knocked on the head in his own back garden, and the family have to change their name. No, sir, I make it a rule of mine: the more it looks like Queer Street, the less I ask."</i><br /><br />When Utterson suspects that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[his friend might be being blackmailed, he makes no mention of it. Neither does he speak out when he thinks Dr. Jekyll might be sheltering Hyde from the police. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> To a Victorian gentleman, his reputation would have been paramount. The unwritten rule of the time, known to all respectable people, stated that one never betrayed a friend, whatever their secret. This may seem hypocrisy to modern eyes, or it may seem loyalty.<br /><br />As the story moves on the relationship between the two is compounded, but it is not until the final chapters, which consist of two letters to be opened in the event of a death, that the horrific story unfolds. This is a popular device of the time, but it lacks immediacy, and the story seems to finish unexpectedly, at the end of one letter, without any sort of conclusion. The descriptions however are very powerful,<br /><br /><i>"As I looked there came, I thought a change - he seemed to swell - his face became suddenly black and the features seemed to melt and alter..." <br /><br />"The most racking pangs succeeded: a grinding in the bones, deadly nausea, and a horror of the spirit that cannot be exceeded at the hour of birth or death. Then these agonies began swiftly to subside, and I came to myself as if out of a great sickness. There was something strange in my sensations, something indescribably sweet. I felt younger, lighter, happier in body; within I was conscious of a heady recklessness, a current of disordered sensual images running like a millrace in my fancy, a solution of the bonds of obligation, an unknown but innocent freedom of the soul. I knew myself, at the first breath of this new life, to be more wicked, tenfold more wicked, sold a slave to my original evil and the thought, in that moment, braced and delighted me like wine." <br /><br />"This was the shocking thing; that the slime of the pit seemed to utter cries and voices; that the amorphous dust gesticulated and sinned; that what was dead, and had no shape, should usurp the offices of life. And this again, that that insurgent horror was knit to him closer than a wife, closer than an eye; lay caged in his flesh, where he heard it mutter and felt it struggle to be born; and at every hour of weakness, and in the confidence of slumber, prevailed against him, and deposed him out of life."</i><br /><br />It is an interesting depiction by Stevenson, that Dr. Jekyll could rarely bring himself to use the personal pronoun when talking about Hyde's most despicable crimes. Indeed, the character makes the same observation himself, yet at first he had talked in the first person throughout.<br /><br />To a modern reader then, this is a story about a split personality, or what is technically called "dissociative identity disorder". But Stevenson also invites us to view it as a moral tale, an allegory, questioning the abstract notions of good and evil. Do we all have a "dark side"? Do we truly have both a tendency to evil and an inclination towards virtue within our natures? If so, how do we decide which is uppermost? Can we consciously control them at all? And which, if either, might continue after death? <br /><br />The author poses the question, leaving it to the reader to decide, although there are hints that he views us all as having a dual nature,<br /><br /><i>“The bargain might appear unequal; but there was still another consideration in the scales; for while Jekyll would suffer smartingly in the fires of abstinence, Hyde would be not even conscious of all that he had lost.”</i> <br /><br />It is always interesting to read the original of a much-loved tale. This has flaws of construction, but is well worth a look even so.<br /><br /><b>EDIT:</b> (a few months later)<br /><br />I've been aware that this is probably worth a little more than my default rating, if only because of its phenomenal influence on popular culture, and writing about this theme, since. So I'm altering my rating to a 4 stars, as it falls somewhere between the two, I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="752385113579625459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer831815255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11415704263811567037">A veeeeeeeery short buddy-read with: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1237794510?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'> Buddy Loooooove</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1259723521?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Too much Buddy Love aka I want to be called The Nutty Professor</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4268487-dustin' rel='nofollow noopener'>I love everybody Buddy Love</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56401535?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>What did I do to deserve this Buddy Love?</a>,<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/849397427?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'> Gimmie some Buddy Love</a>....aaaaaand <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1246928-holly' rel='nofollow noopener'>My brand new Buddy Love</a>. Whew! Did I get everyone??? <br/><br/>I am not a classic book reader- I fall under the category that some snobbish readers would call a fluffy reader..a reader for entertainment purposes only- Not a reader for intellectual growth. The classics were read in m</span>
  <span id="freeText11415704263811567037" style="display:none">A veeeeeeeery short buddy-read with: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1237794510?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener"> Buddy Loooooove</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1259723521?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Too much Buddy Love aka I want to be called The Nutty Professor</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4268487-dustin" rel="nofollow noopener">I love everybody Buddy Love</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56401535?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">What did I do to deserve this Buddy Love?</a>,<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/849397427?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener"> Gimmie some Buddy Love</a>....aaaaaand <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1246928-holly" rel="nofollow noopener">My brand new Buddy Love</a>. Whew! Did I get everyone??? <br><br>I am not a classic book reader- I fall under the category that some snobbish readers would call a fluffy reader..a reader for entertainment purposes only- Not a reader for intellectual growth. The classics were read in my high school and college years- and I was soooooo burned out by the time I finished the ones I HAD to read- I just wanted fuuuuuuuuuun in my spare time...but when I heard that my favorite Goodreaders were picking this one up....and was also aware that- this one was sooooooo itsy bitsy..I knew I had to join in. Maybe I could be smart and have fuuuuuuuuuuun at the same time.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431020426i/14787222.jpg" alt=" photo a97d3047-a18f-4ed8-b3bf-c8cf96207eaf_zpsk6g1f2tu.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The sensible lawyer- Mr. Utterson- listens as his long time friend- Enfield tells a sinister tale.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431020426i/14787223.jpg" alt=" photo hyframe_zpsdzbdziwc.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>He speaks of a wicked figure named Mr. Hyde- who assaulted a young girl and then quickly disappeared and re-appeared- only to make payment to her family. Mr. Utterson has heard this name before...for another of his close friends (and clients)- Dr. Jekyll, made a will that will leave his property to this same horrible man.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431020426i/14787224.jpg" alt=" photo hydeframe_zpsv33xwmex.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Short...but packs a punch! I realllllllllllllly liked this- and I have to stop avoiding some of these novels I have written off as "too serious for me". Highly recommended for anyone that has a couple of hours to spare.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11415704263811567037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8098204185938184774"><b>“Man is not truly one, but truly two” </b><br/><br/>Dr. Jekyll attains through his experience with being both himself and Mr. Hyde that there are actually two sides to him. He claims that people, as far as he can tell, are made up of two sides― good and evil.<br/> <br/><i>But is it really true? </i><br/><br/>Is a good and dignified person, good and dignified because that is what s/he is supposed to be?<br/>Because that is the way s/he is expected to be?<br/><br/>Does everyone have a secret dark side that they desperately keep in their closet?   <span id="freeText8098204185938184774" style="display:none"><b>“Man is not truly one, but truly two” </b><br /><br />Dr. Jekyll attains through his experience with being both himself and Mr. Hyde that there are actually two sides to him. He claims that people, as far as he can tell, are made up of two sides― good and evil.<br /> <br /><i>But is it really true? </i><br /><br />Is a good and dignified person, good and dignified because that is what s/he is supposed to be?<br />Because that is the way s/he is expected to be?<br /><br />Does everyone have a secret dark side that they desperately keep in their closet? Does this side hide in the dark, lurks, and waits for the perfect opportunity to be unleashed? <br /><br />What happens when this dark side― along with its impulses― is repressed?<br />Does it create some sort of duality―two personalities that seem opposite and antagonistic? <br /><br />Is the conflict between the two sides Man's burden till the last breath s/he takes? What happens when you dwell on a side and neglect the other? Is a certain balance possible?<br /><br /><br /><b>“All human beings, as we meet them, are commingled out of good and evil: and Edward Hyde, alone, in the ranks of mankind, was pure evil.” <br /><br /><br />“I have been made to learn that the doom and burden of our life is bound forever on man’s shoulders; and when the attempt is made to cast it off, it but returns upon us with more unfamiliar and more awful pressure.” <br /><br /><br />“You must suffer me to go my own dark way.” </b></span>

<div id="review_2990284907" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2990284907" />
    <a title="Susan Budd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44131812-susan-budd"><img alt="Susan Budd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434841111p2/44131812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2990284907?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Budd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Budd" href="/user/show/44131812-susan-budd">Susan Budd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451532251" href="/book/show/13585761-dr-jekyll-and-mr-hyde">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44131812-susan-budd?shelf=english-literature-uk">english-literature-uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2990284907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18365668830716957026">I had to read <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> a few times before I could expel the legion of Dr. Jekylls and Mr. Hydes that infested my imagination. Countless pop culture references have robbed the story of the suspense and surprise that early readers must have enjoyed. But suspense and surprise are cheap pleasures compared to the richness that lies in the text. <br/><br/>Stevenson has written a perfect nightmare. Everything about the story is dream-like. It begins with Utterson crossing into liminal space while </span>
  <span id="freeText18365668830716957026" style="display:none">I had to read <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> a few times before I could expel the legion of Dr. Jekylls and Mr. Hydes that infested my imagination. Countless pop culture references have robbed the story of the suspense and surprise that early readers must have enjoyed. But suspense and surprise are cheap pleasures compared to the richness that lies in the text. <br /><br />Stevenson has written a perfect nightmare. Everything about the story is dream-like. It begins with Utterson crossing into liminal space while taking a Sunday afternoon stroll with his cousin Enfield. One moment they are passing bright, clean, inviting shop fronts. The next, they come upon a dark and squalid building. Utterson doesn’t know it yet, but he has just entered the Twilight Zone. <br /><br />From this point on, the atmosphere is relentlessly and preternaturally gloomy. There’s a perpetual fog. The labyrinthine streets are sinister in the gaslight. This is not just nineteenth century London. This is a “<i>city in a nightmare</i>” (71). <br /><br />The night after a brutal murder, Utterson escorts the police to Hyde’s house. It is nine o’clock in the morning, but it might as well be nighttime. Even when the darkness lifts, it is brief. And these glimpses of light only serve to better highlight the darkness. The story of the Carew murder evokes more horror because it follows an illusory return to light and ordinary life.<br /><br />Like a dream, <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> is many-layered. There are dreams within the dream. Narratives within the narrative. Communication is oblique. Lanyon will not speak to Utterson about Jekyll. Jekyll will not reveal his reasons for avoiding Utterson. When Lanyon and Jekyll finally tell their stories, it is from the grave.<br /><br />Utterson both keeps secrets and has secrets kept from him. The secrecy and silence create a mental claustrophobia. The anxiety of not knowing what is going on. The anxiety of knowing too much. Of being dangerously close to the arcane machinery that lurks behind the façade of the workaday and ordinary. His isolation is palpable.<br /><br />Also like a dream, no time seems to pass between the scenes. It is simply a “<i>fortnight later</i>” (65). “<i>Nearly a year later</i>” (68). Scenes recur. A second Sunday stroll leads Utterson and Enfield to Jekyll’s window just as their stroll had previously led them to Hyde’s door. <br /><br />Windows and doors are liminal places. Portals into other worlds. Private worlds. Forbidden worlds. Utterson and Enfield see Jekyll at his window at the moment he begins his involuntary transformation into Hyde. A maid looks through her window and sees Hyde murder Carew. <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> begins with Hyde’s door and ends with Jekyll’s door. <br /><br />The pièce de résistance of this nightmare is the nebulous yet persistent impression that something isn’t quite right about Hyde. He inspires repugnance in everyone who sees him. Enfield says it best: “<i>he gives a strong feeling of deformity, although I couldn’t specify the point</i>” (53). Though reason cannot specify the point, something deeper and more instinctual within us recoils in disgust and horror. <br /><br />When I first read <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i>, I read it as a psychological and moral story. Jekyll’s story. But later it was the dream-like quality of the book that took hold of my imagination. Then I read it as a nightmare. And it is Utterson’s nightmare, not Jekyll’s. Everything in a dream is so much more than it seems. And that is true of Stevenson’s nightmare as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18365668830716957026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1248608657" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1248608657" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1248608657?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1248608657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8519205590892467229">The story is widely known and very influential. It was retold and replayed countless number of times by practically everywhere and everybody, including one of the best cartoon series of all the time, Looney Tunes:<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475174588i/20692141._SX540_.jpg' width='480' height='359' alt='Looney Tunes' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>For this reason people writing blurbs for the book decided it is quite fine to take a lazy route and give spoiler right away. At least in my opinion something revealed only in the last chapter should be considered a spoiler. <br/> <br/>I am going to assume there are people who have no clue what t</span>
  <span id="freeText8519205590892467229" style="display:none">The story is widely known and very influential. It was retold and replayed countless number of times by practically everywhere and everybody, including one of the best cartoon series of all the time, Looney Tunes:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475174588i/20692141._SX540_.jpg" width="480" height="359" alt="Looney Tunes" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>For this reason people writing blurbs for the book decided it is quite fine to take a lazy route and give spoiler right away. At least in my opinion something revealed only in the last chapter should be considered a spoiler. <br> <br>I am going to assume there are people who have no clue what the book is about and only tell the very beginning without revealing the contents of the aforementioned last chapter. Imagine a typical old-fashioned respected Victorian doctor:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475174620i/20692153._SY540_.jpg" width="373" height="480" alt="Victorian doctor" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>He lived a typical for his class life when his friends began noticing his mysterious connection to a highly disagreeable (I am trying to use the appropriate for that time term) man called Mr. Hyde. The first obvious conclusion was a blackmail - it seems a good doctor led a fairly wild life when he was a youth. Once again let me remind you that most probably his life was wild only in the eyes of his Victorian contemporaries. So it seems Mr. Hyde knew something about the doctor because the latter never failed to hush up the crazy adventures of the former. The truth turned out to be much more gruesome. <br> <br>I would not qualify the book as horror as it is not scary. It does have a great atmosphere though and a couple of scenes are quite spooky. The writing style while somewhat aged is still quite good and makes an easy read. <br> <br>Having said this I need to mention I was really bored by the end. Why? The tale has a clear message; it was so clear I would not even talk about it to avoid spoilers for those rare individuals who do not know the story. Anyhow, by the end I had a strong impression that the delivering of the message was a little heavy-handed. I am not trying to tell the author was driving it home with a hammer; far from it. He was using more serious tool for this:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475174651i/20692154._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="163" alt="Sledgehammer" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This made reading the last chapter quite a chore with the only saving grace being the overall length of the book - it is fairly short. This is the reason why I lowered my rating for otherwise classic horror story: <b>3.5 stars</b> rounded down. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8519205590892467229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2357390236" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2357390236" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2357390236?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4833988093358592093">Bruce Banner / The Hulk, Lawrence Talbot / The Wolfman, and Norman Bates are watching Stanley Kubrick’s 1987 film Full Metal Jacket and Joker has just said to the visiting Colonel that his helmet decorations were meant “to suggest something about the duality of man”.<br/><br/>Norman: We all go a little mad sometimes.<br/><br/>Bruce: This makes me think of Robert Louis Stevenson’s 1886 novella The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde. I mean about the whole duality of man thing.<br/><br/>Talbot: I think that is a ubiquitou</span>
  <span id="freeText4833988093358592093" style="display:none">Bruce Banner / The Hulk, Lawrence Talbot / The Wolfman, and Norman Bates are watching Stanley Kubrick’s 1987 film Full Metal Jacket and Joker has just said to the visiting Colonel that his helmet decorations were meant “to suggest something about the duality of man”.<br><br>Norman: We all go a little mad sometimes.<br><br>Bruce: This makes me think of Robert Louis Stevenson’s 1886 novella The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde. I mean about the whole duality of man thing.<br><br>Talbot: I think that is a ubiquitous element of much of fiction, especially in the fantasy or horror genres, that someone can be two people at once, or can change from a civilized man into a monster.<br><br>Norman: Stephen King observed this in his treatise on horror fiction <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11563.Danse_Macabre" title="Danse Macabre by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Danse Macabre</a>, that one of the basic tenants of horror, one of the fundamental templates for a horror story is the idea that we can cross a line and become a fiend.<br><br>Talbot: My own unique situation with lycanthropy is a study in this, as is my inner struggle about how enjoyable it is to become the wolf, to set aside the morals of society and be a beast. <br><br>Bruce: But like Jekyll, the consequences of the beastly behavior becomes too overwhelming when we return to human. I feel a tremendous responsibility to control the Hulk, to minimize the damage that he / we / I can cause. I think that is part of what destroyed Jekyll. I must control my anger, you wouldn’t like me when I’m angry.<br><br>Norman: This concept, this idea goes back to mythology, with the Roman god Janus and of the personification of transitions and duality, the idea that we represent opposing forces, divergent walkers on the same path. My mother likes to say things like that anyway.<br><br>Bruce: Interestingly, and opposed to much of the adaptations of this story, Hyde was described as “dwarfish” and not bigger than Jekyll; most modern interpretations of this story show him as a larger than life, hulking figure whereas Stevenson drew him as smaller.<br><br>Norman: Perhaps Stevenson was eliciting a more Victorian ideal about decadence, depravity and the diminution of societal norms, being monstrous made him “less” of a man, he was dealing in metaphor.<br><br>Talbot: An intriguing story and a must read for fans of the horror genre as it represents a fundamental pattern in this context.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554554333i/27323749._SY540_.jpg" width="259" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="4833988093358592093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer1143069826441128157"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br/>4 out of 5 stars to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51496.The_Strange_Case_of_Dr__Jekyll_and_Mr__Hyde' title='The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde by Robert Louis Stevenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</a> written in 1886 by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/854076.Robert_Louis_Stevenson' title='Robert Louis Stevenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robert Louis Stevenson</a>. So here's how naive I was years ago... and keep in mind I was an English major who loved the classics... I'd read some short stories about Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde as a teenager, maybe saw some video or tv versions.... can't quite remember. Sophomore year in college, this is listed on the assigned syllabus for one of my courses. And I'm like "I think there's a mistake.</span>
  <span id="freeText1143069826441128157" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br />4 out of 5 stars to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51496.The_Strange_Case_of_Dr__Jekyll_and_Mr__Hyde" title="The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde by Robert Louis Stevenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</a> written in 1886 by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/854076.Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>. So here's how naive I was years ago... and keep in mind I was an English major who loved the classics... I'd read some short stories about Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde as a teenager, maybe saw some video or tv versions.... can't quite remember. Sophomore year in college, this is listed on the assigned syllabus for one of my courses. And I'm like "I think there's a mistake. Stevenson wrote Treasure Island. He didn't create this mystery about a strange man." I couldn't separate that the author had different styles and stories. I don't know what I was thinking... maybe I had no sleep... point being, this was a turning point in literature for me, where I realized how an author could truly write very different novels. And both be great! For me, this was why I loved reading all the time. Drama. Intrigue. Mystery. Suspense. Crazy. Unique. Peculiar. It was everything my boring life wasn't at the time. I suspect most people don't realize this was a lengthy novel before it was a short work and a TV thing. It's a must read. Go. Now.<br /><br /><b>

And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. Leave a comment and let me know what you think. Vote in the poll and ratings. <span id="freeTextContainer14343816796833105594">Review in English and in Spanish<br/><br/>This story is full of suspense, quite intriguing.   <span id="freeText14343816796833105594" style="display:none">Review in English and in Spanish<br /><br />This story is full of suspense, quite intriguing. I hope that those who have decided to read this great classic will also take the time to read even more about its author and how this novel arises for him and for the world we know today. The most interesting things for me is the game of the duality of human consciousness something that can be seen in Greek and Roman mythology, but in this time still full of dark light, declaring that we are a combination of black and white and not one or the other. Love this story and I recommend it too.<br /><br />Esta historia esta llena de suspenso, bastante intrigante. Espero a su vez que los que hallan decidió leer este gran clásico se tomen el tiempo de leer aun mas de su autor y como surge esta novel para el y para el mundo que hoy conocemos. De las cosas mas interesantes para mi es el juego de la dualidad de la conciencia humana algo que se puede evidenciar en la mitología griega y romana, pero que atreves del tiempo sigue siendo la misma llena de claros oscuros, declarando que somos una combinación entre lo negro y lo blanco y no lo uno u lo otro. Ame esta historia y la recomiendo demasiado. </span>

<div id="review_1349443" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1349443" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1349443?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=19th-c-brit">19th-c-brit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=novella">novella</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1349443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14730831515909670952"><br/>The cabinets, purchased by his father, had been in his home for as long as young Robert could remember; nevertheless, he continued to wonder, staring at them from time to time. Not because they were fine examples of the cabinet-maker’s art—although indeed they were excellent cabinets—but because they had been crafted by the hands and the tools of the notorious Deacon Brodie.<br/><br/>William “Deacon” Brodie had long been a household name in Scotland, particularly in Edinburgh, where young Robert Louis Ste</span>
  <span id="freeText14730831515909670952" style="display:none"><br />The cabinets, purchased by his father, had been in his home for as long as young Robert could remember; nevertheless, he continued to wonder, staring at them from time to time. Not because they were fine examples of the cabinet-maker’s art—although indeed they were excellent cabinets—but because they had been crafted by the hands and the tools of the notorious Deacon Brodie.<br /><br />William “Deacon” Brodie had long been a household name in Scotland, particularly in Edinburgh, where young Robert Louis Stevenson lived. He was known to his fellow citizens as a pillar of the community: a gifted and respected cabinet maker, the head (“deacon”) of the local cabinet-makers’ guild (“The Incorporation of Wrights”), and a member of the Edinburgh town council.<br /><br />But there was another side to Deacon Brodie, one he endeavored to hide. He kept two mistresses, supported five children, and was an inveterate gambler who loved nothing more than a good cockfight, a pack of cards, or a pair of dice. As he often found himself in need of money, he decided to supplement his daily income with a furtive night-time employment: he masterminded a gang of burglars who broke into the houses of Edinburgh. (Here his daytime trade came in handy, for a cabinet-maker—in Brodie’s day—was expected to be an accomplished locksmith as well.) Eventually the deacon was caught, and hanged for his crimes, on the same gallows—so the legend says—he had recently purchased on behalf of the town council.<br /><br />As he stared at his father’s old cabinets, young Robert Louis Stevenson contemplated the mystery of the cabinets’ maker: how could such an upright public man, he wondered, be a hidden slough of evil underneath? As a teenager, Stevenson wrote a dramatic fragment about the Deacon, and in his thirties—with the help of his friend W. E. Henley—turned that early effort into a play. But old Brodie continued to haunt our author until in 1885—after a vivid dream adorned with night-terrors—the deacon was transformed into the celebrated Dr. Jekyll and his nocturnal double Mr. Hyde.<br /><br />The ghost of Deacon Brodie and his two professions still hovers over Stevenson’s completed narrative, for the tale of that poor bifurcated gentleman Jekyll/Hyde is filled with: doors with limited access, doors that must be broken into, doors and drawers opened with mysterious keys, or else by locksmiths pressed into service, and drawers which must be taken from their cupboards and removed—contents and all—from their proper residences. Oh, and Jekyll’s medical theater features a small private room—home to the aforementioned cupboards and drawers—which is designated by the word the Victorians applied to such rooms: it is referred to as Dr. Jekyll’s “cabinet."<br /><br />And the structure of <i>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> resembles a cabinet too. It consists of ten drawers—or chapters—the top eight of which each contains an isolated piece of lawyer Utterson’s reminiscences, and the bottom two the manuscripts which constitute the real “treasure” of the piece: the narrative of Jekyll’s mentor Dr. Lanyon, and the “statement” of Dr. Jekyll himself.<br /><br />It is an appropriate structure, after all, for the story of a man like Jekyll, who desired above all to maintain the purity of self by separating his identity into compartments: one drawer for the absolutely good Dr. Jekyll, and one drawer for the perfectly evil Mr. Hyde. It is also fitting—and ironic—that Dr. Jekyll eventually discovers that it was <i>an unknown impurity in the original “salt” (chemical compound) that effected his transformation into Hyde</i>, and therefore made the reversal of the process is practically impossible. As Jekyll tells Lanyon in his concluding statement:<blockquote>
  <i>My provision of the salt, which had never been renewed since the date of the first experiment, began to run low. I sent out for a fresh supply, and mixed the draught; the ebullition followed, and the first change of colour, not the second; I drank it and it was without efficiency. You will learn from Poole how I have had London ransacked; it was in vain; and I am now persuaded that my first supply was impure, and that it was that unknown impurity which lent efficacy to the draught. About a week has passed, and I am now finishing this statement under the influence of the last of the old powders. This, then, is the last time, short of a miracle, that Henry Jekyll can think his own thoughts or see his own face (now how sadly altered!) in the glass.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14730831515909670952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1408799488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408799488" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408799488?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408799488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6673852002540800416"><i>“I have become a monster! I must find a place where I can hide! That’s it! I shall call myself…” DUN-DUN-DUUUUN!!! <br/>“Mr. Where-I-can!”</i><br/><br/>The above is paraphrased from a “Morecambe & Wise” <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> sketch, they don’t often make me laugh, but this one is gold!<br/><br/>Not so much "The Strange Case" as the "Overly Familiar Case". <i>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> is one of those stories that practically everybody knows so few people bother to read the original text. The original <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein' title='Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'> Franke</a></span>
  <span id="freeText6673852002540800416" style="display:none"><i>“I have become a monster! I must find a place where I can hide! That’s it! I shall call myself…” DUN-DUN-DUUUUN!!! <br>“Mr. Where-I-can!”</i><br><br>The above is paraphrased from a “Morecambe &amp; Wise” <i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> sketch, they don’t often make me laugh, but this one is gold!<br><br>Not so much "The Strange Case" as the "Overly Familiar Case". <i>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> is one of those stories that practically everybody knows so few people bother to read the original text. The original <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener"> Frankenstein</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17245.Dracula" title="Dracula by Bram Stoker" rel="nofollow noopener"> Dracula</a> are also often neglected by readers for the same reason. This is a shame because these are great books and well worth reading, (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener"> Frankenstein</a> is particularly beautifully written).<br><br>Clearly the inspiration for “Dr. Banner and Mr. Hulk”, <i>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> is, first and foremost, a damn fine horror story. If you ignore the fact that you already know all the plot points and just immerse yourself into Robert Louis Stevenson’s wonderfully atmospheric setting and prose. Imagine walking around a foggy London street in Victorian times, whistling some spooky tune, and suddenly — DUN-DUN-DUUUUN!!! — Mr. Hyde comes out of nowhere and whacks you on the head.<br><br>The theme of the duality of human nature is not exactly vague since it takes on a such a physical manifestation. However, Stevenson leaves you to draw your own conclusion of whether Jekyll’s theory is valid. The story is also an allegory and a cautionary tale for inebriation (or getting wasted), and yielding to temptation in general. “Just one more pint” and you may find yourself whacking people in foggy London.<br><br>Interestingly Dr. Jekyll is not as good a guy as many people may assume. The text clearly indicates that he is always up for a wild time, painting the town red, visiting houses of ill repute, and doing some serious S&amp;M*. Besides, no decent gentleman is going to deliberately — and repeatedly — take drugs that turn him into a psychopath.<br><br>Anyway, do give <i>The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i> a read, it may be old hat, but it is a headwear never goes out of fashion. Try it on for size!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445017475i/16587562._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Art by "<a href="http://mb-cg.deviantart.com/art/Dr-Jekyll-and-Mr-Hyde-338190576" rel="nofollow noopener">MB-CG</a>".<br>_________________<br><br>* Not to be confused with M&amp;S which is Marks &amp; Spencer, where you can be fairly sure of non-mayhem.<br><br><br><u>Notes</u>:<br>• Cool quote:<br><i>"Will you be wise? will you be guided? will you suffer me to take this glass in my hand and to go forth from your house without further parley? or has the greed of curiosity too much command of you?"</i><br><br>• There are several audiobook versions of this book on Librivox, I chose <a href="https://librivox.org/the-strange-case-of-dr-jekyll-mr-hyde-by-robert-louis-stevenson/" rel="nofollow noopener">the one read by David Barnes</a>, as he sounds suitably English. The narration is a little bit of a monotone, but nice and clearly read, and it's free so I can't complain. Thank you Mr. Barnes!<br><br>• Are you reading this review in October? Definitely add it to your Halloween list!<br><br>• If you like "Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde" check out <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/746007.The_Bottle_Imp" title="The Bottle Imp by Robert Louis Stevenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bottle Imp</a> by the same author. It is an awesome supernatural short story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6673852002540800416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2041002217" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2041002217" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2041002217?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140623505" href="/book/show/6443632-dr-jekyll-and-mr-hyde">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=1001-before-you-die">1001-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=english">english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2041002217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16487128786314573854">My impetus for reading this classic 1886 novella was seeing an interview with Donna Tartt in which she discusses writing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333223.The_Goldfinch' title='The Goldfinch by Donna Tartt' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Goldfinch</a>. She says that she read Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde during her formative years and that there's "something of it in every book I've ever written". Well, since I adore every book Ms. Tartt has ever written, it was high time I read this.<br/><br/>I love the creeptastic gothic stories from this time period - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein' title='Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Frankenstein</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17245.Dracula' title='Dracula by Bram Stoker' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dracula</a>, anything by Poe. There's something dank and dingy i</span>
  <span id="freeText16487128786314573854" style="display:none">My impetus for reading this classic 1886 novella was seeing an interview with Donna Tartt in which she discusses writing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333223.The_Goldfinch" title="The Goldfinch by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Goldfinch</a>. She says that she read Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde during her formative years and that there's "something of it in every book I've ever written". Well, since I adore every book Ms. Tartt has ever written, it was high time I read this.<br /><br />I love the creeptastic gothic stories from this time period - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Frankenstein</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17245.Dracula" title="Dracula by Bram Stoker" rel="nofollow noopener">Dracula</a>, anything by Poe. There's something dank and dingy in those pages that makes the skin turn clammy. It must have been highly original for the time, and made me realise that Stevenson's story has influenced and inspired many writers through the years, not just Tartt. (Hello, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5759.Fight_Club" title="Fight Club by Chuck Palahniuk" rel="nofollow noopener">Fight Club</a>?) The execution of this book wasn't necessarily my cup of tea; a few written post mortem confessions don't exactly bring horror to life. BUT <i>the ideas</i> behind this book are what really interest me. Duplicity, shame, alienation, and morality, to name a few. It is chilling to acknowledge we all have a set of "polar twins" that are "continuously struggling" in order to both satisfy self and present well in society. That each person has their own Hyde scratching in the basement is everyone's dirty little secret.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16487128786314573854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_977174346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/977174346" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/977174346?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer977174346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17815051729895576674">"I learned to recognise the thorough and primitive duality of man; I saw that, of the two natures that contended in the field of my consciousness, even if I could rightly be said to be either, it was only because I was radically both."<br/><br/>As so often, my students gave me food for thought after I carelessly summed up the idea behind Doctor Jekyll and Mister Hyde and the duality of humankind, moving between animal brutality and intellectual sophistication.<br/><br/>"But that is not true!"<br/><br/>Of course I thought the</span>
  <span id="freeText17815051729895576674" style="display:none">"I learned to recognise the thorough and primitive duality of man; I saw that, of the two natures that contended in the field of my consciousness, even if I could rightly be said to be either, it was only because I was radically both."<br /><br />As so often, my students gave me food for thought after I carelessly summed up the idea behind Doctor Jekyll and Mister Hyde and the duality of humankind, moving between animal brutality and intellectual sophistication.<br /><br />"But that is not true!"<br /><br />Of course I thought the next argument would be that people are not divided and that the story is exaggerated, and of course I was wrong.<br /><br />"People are not dual, they are multiple personalities, that idea is way to0 easy!"<br /><br />So the teacher in me stayed quiet for a moment and let the student and learner in me consider the piece of evidence presented to me by a learner-turned-teacher in front of me. Then I nodded. It's true, we are multiple, all of us, and we are much more versatile in our metamorphosis from one personality to another than Stevenson captured in his famous story. We don't even need to manipulate our organism to change - we do it instantly when we face another human being. In school, I am a certain person that completely disappears when I am a patient at a hospital, and my mother persona does not follow my body to the pub when I meet friends. My daughter persona actually acts in a much younger way than my default persona sitting in a reading chair imagining to be a character in a fiction story...<br /><br />And it is not only behaviour. Looks change too. Watch people queueing in a supermarket, and compare them to themselves at a wedding. Is it not a case of Doctor Jekyll and Mr Hyde? <br /><br />So I revise my idea of Stevenson's story, without liking it any less, and claim it is a simplification of the crowd we all carry in our minds, - the microcosm of thought we let loose on the macrocosm of other minds each day!<br /><br />My Goodreader persona could stay forever in front of the screen, but the coffee devil inside is yelling something animalistic about an addiction he's forced upon the community of minds that my tired Wednesday morning body is hosting!<br /><br />So we're off to the coffee machine!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17815051729895576674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3590773790" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3590773790" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464987365p2/16254355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3590773790?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781400158591" href="/book/show/7174647-the-strange-case-of-dr-jekyll-and-mr-hyde">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3590773790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6653074760628726160">This is a classic book that I could not remember if I have read before or not. I know the story and have seen many different uses, references to it, retellings, etc. My favorite is probably Angry Video Game nerd on YouTube who HATES the video game version from the original Nintendo. However, now I have 100% for sure read it!<br/><br/>This has a similar format to other Victorian horror novels: lots of letters and retellings from some of the main characters and some bystanders. This format is well known fro</span>
  <span id="freeText6653074760628726160" style="display:none">This is a classic book that I could not remember if I have read before or not. I know the story and have seen many different uses, references to it, retellings, etc. My favorite is probably Angry Video Game nerd on YouTube who HATES the video game version from the original Nintendo. However, now I have 100% for sure read it!<br /><br />This has a similar format to other Victorian horror novels: lots of letters and retellings from some of the main characters and some bystanders. This format is well known from the books Dracula and Frankenstein. In fact, when I was done, I tried to remember if the reader ever actually meets Dr. Jekyll, or if he is just referenced in other peoples' anecdotes.<br /><br />It is a decent, quick read. Maybe not really all that scary or horrific (if that is what you are looking for). But, the mystery is a bit spooky and the characters interesting. Whether you like it or not, it will only take two or three hours of your life and then you can check another classic off your list (and, you can then see how it compares to all the other references you have seen to it!)<br /><br />Click below for Angry Video Game Nerd's take on Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://youtu.be/EjXn5qiM8Zw">https://youtu.be/EjXn5qiM8Zw</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6653074760628726160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gY7BeF6BErBQ5gvzquQNzRtg8WSCDSFwdpq+4Q713CkDYnd5XbLCQVcTYc5+HBoiGcpJOa/nktmF2ZkeZNVLnA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('unHldE8hpTX/Ev3tQ4vMCbBHFCs/yvrpfsYWCGeNDhY4nVN1TBJ1xPjnl9CXc9vmsu2sdhIgSUCNhTH3Da2Zow==')})
  }

They wanted <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17988939302201201112\" style=\"display:none\">Ranked among the classic novels of the English language and the inspiration for several unforgettable movies, this early work of H. G. Wells was greeted in 1896 by howls of protest from reviewers, who found it horrifying and blasphemous. They wanted to know more about the wondrous possibilities of science shown in his first book, The Time Machine, not its potential for misuse and terror. In The Island of Dr. Moreau, a shipwrecked gentleman named Edward Prendick, stranded on a Pacific island lorded over by the notorious Dr. Moreau, confronts dark secrets, strange creatures, and a reason to run for his life.\n\nWhile this riveting tale was intended to be a commentary on evolution, divine creation, and the tension between human nature and culture, modern readers familiar with genetic engineering will marvel at Wells’s prediction of the ethical issues raised by producing “smarter” human beings or bringing back extinct species. These levels of interpretation add a richness to Prendick’s adventures on Dr. Moreau’s island of lost souls without distracting from what is still a rip-roaring good read.<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9276680164356900990">Robert Louis Balfour Stevenson was a Scottish novelist, poet, and travel writer, and a leading representative of English literature. He was greatly admired by many authors, including Jorge Luis Borges, Ernest Hemingway, Rudyard Kipling and Vladimir Nabokov.<br/><br/>Most modernist writers dismissed him, however, because he was popular and did not write within their narrow definition of literature. It is onl</span>
  <span id="freeText9276680164356900990" style="display:none">Robert Louis Balfour Stevenson was a Scottish novelist, poet, and travel writer, and a leading representative of English literature. He was greatly admired by many authors, including Jorge Luis Borges, Ernest Hemingway, Rudyard Kipling and Vladimir Nabokov.<br /><br />Most modernist writers dismissed him, however, because he was popular and did not write within their narrow definition of literature. It is only recently that critics have begun to look beyond Stevenson's popularity and allow him a place in the Western canon.</span>
